<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Ancient Mesopotamian civilization</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Sumeria" redirects here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Sumer_(disambiguation)" title="Sumer (disambiguation)">Sumer (disambiguation)</a> and <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Sumeria_(disambiguation)" title="Sumeria (disambiguation)">Sumeria (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Summer" title="Summer">Summer</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

class="infobox-label" scope="row">Dates</th><td class="infobox-data"><abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">‚Äâ4500</span>¬†‚Äì c.<span style="white-space:nowrap;">‚Äâ1900 BC</span></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Preceded by</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Ubaid_period" title="Ubaid period">Ubaid period</a></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Followed by</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Akkadian_Empire" title="Akkadian Empire">Akkadian Empire</a></td></tr></tbody></table><p><b>Sumer</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/Àà/: primary stress follows">Àà</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/uÀê/: 'oo' in 'goose'">uÀê</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/…ôr/: 'er' in 'letter'">…ôr</span></span>/</a></span></span>) is the earliest known <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">civilization</a> in the historical region of southern <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> (south-central <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>), emerging during the <a href="/wiki/Chalcolithic" title="Chalcolithic">Chalcolithic</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Bronze_Age" title="Early Bronze Age">early Bronze</a> Ages between the sixth and fifth millennium BC. It is one of the <a href="/wiki/Cradle_of_civilization" title="Cradle of civilization">cradles of civilization</a> in the world, along with <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">ancient Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elam" title="Elam">Elam</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Caral-Supe_civilization" title="Caral-Supe civilization">Caral-Supe civilization</a>, <a href="/wiki/Olmecs" title="Olmecs">Olmecs</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_civilisation" title="Indus Valley civilisation">Indus Valley civilisation</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_China" title="Ancient China">ancient China</a>. Living along the valleys of the <a href="/wiki/Tigris" title="Tigris">Tigris</a> and <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a> rivers, Sumerian farmers grew an abundance of grain and other crops, the surplus from which enabled them to form urban settlements. <a href="/wiki/Proto-writing" title="Proto-writing">Proto-writing</a> dates back before 3000 BC. The earliest texts come from the cities of <a href="/wiki/Uruk" title="Uruk">Uruk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jemdet_Nasr" title="Jemdet Nasr">Jemdet Nasr</a>, and date to between c. 3500 and c. 3000 BC.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag is ambiguous, and needs clarification. (May 2022)">ambiguous</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Left: Sculpture of the head of Sumerian ruler <a href="/wiki/Gudea" title="Gudea">Gudea</a>, c. 2150 BC. Right: <a href="/wiki/Cuneiform" title="Cuneiform">cuneiform</a> characters for <i>Saƒù-g√≠g</i> (<span lang="und-Xsux" style="font-size:125%;font-family:'Segoe UI Historic','Akkadian','Noto Sans Cuneiform','Noto Sans Sumero-Akkadian Cuneiform';" title="cuneiform text"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%F0%92%8A%95" title="wikt:íäï">íäï</a></span> <span lang="und-Xsux" style="font-size:125%;font-family:'Segoe UI Historic','Akkadian','Noto Sans Cuneiform','Noto Sans Sumero-Akkadian Cuneiform';" title="cuneiform text"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%F0%92%88%AA" title="wikt:íà™">íà™</a></span>), "Black Headed Ones", the native designation for the Sumerians. The first is the pictographic character for "head" (<a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sa%C4%9D_(linear_script,_head).jpg"><img alt="Saƒù (linear script, head).jpg" data-file-height="352" data-file-width="274" decoding="async" height="18" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Sa%C4%9D_%28linear_script%2C_head%29.jpg/14px-Sa%C4%9D_%28linear_script%2C_head%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Sa%C4%9D_%28linear_script%2C_head%29.jpg/21px-Sa%C4%9D_%28linear_script%2C_head%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Sa%C4%9D_%28linear_script%2C_head%29.jpg/28px-Sa%C4%9D_%28linear_script%2C_head%29.jpg 2x" width="14"/></a>, later <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sa%C4%9D_(Sumero-Akkadian_cuneiform,_head).jpg"><img alt="Saƒù (Sumero-Akkadian cuneiform, head).jpg" data-file-height="352" data-file-width="237" decoding="async" height="18" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Sa%C4%9D_%28Sumero-Akkadian_cuneiform%2C_head%29.jpg/18px-Sa%C4%9D_%28Sumero-Akkadian_cuneiform%2C_head%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Sa%C4%9D_%28Sumero-Akkadian_cuneiform%2C_head%29.jpg/24px-Sa%C4%9D_%28Sumero-Akkadian_cuneiform%2C_head%29.jpg 2x" width="12"/></a>), the second the character for "night", and for "black" when pronounced <i>g√≠g</i> (<a class="image" href="/wiki/File:G%C3%ADg_(linear_script,_night-black).jpg"><img alt="G√≠g (linear script, night-black).jpg" data-file-height="352" data-file-width="274" decoding="async" height="18" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/G%C3%ADg_%28linear_script%2C_night-black%29.jpg/14px-G%C3%ADg_%28linear_script%2C_night-black%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/G%C3%ADg_%28linear_script%2C_night-black%29.jpg/21px-G%C3%ADg_%28linear_script%2C_night-black%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/G%C3%ADg_%28linear_script%2C_night-black%29.jpg/28px-G%C3%ADg_%28linear_script%2C_night-black%29.jpg 2x" width="14"/></a>, later <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:G%C3%ADg_(Sumero-Akkadian_cuneiform,_night-black).jpg"><img alt="G√≠g (Sumero-Akkadian cuneiform, night-black).jpg" data-file-height="390" data-file-width="266" decoding="async" height="18" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/G%C3%ADg_%28Sumero-Akkadian_cuneiform%2C_night-black%29.jpg/12px-G%C3%ADg_%28Sumero-Akkadian_cuneiform%2C_night-black%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/G%C3%ADg_%28Sumero-Akkadian_cuneiform%2C_night-black%29.jpg/18px-G%C3%ADg_%28Sumero-Akkadian_cuneiform%2C_night-black%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/G%C3%ADg_%28Sumero-Akkadian_cuneiform%2C_night-black%29.jpg/24px-G%C3%ADg_%28Sumero-Akkadian_cuneiform%2C_night-black%29.jpg 2x" width="12"/></a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAF_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-DAF-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PUSD_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-PUSD-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IMD_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-IMD-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GF_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-GF-4">[4]</a></sup></div></div></div></div>
<p>The term "Sumer" (<a href="/wiki/Sumerian_language" title="Sumerian language">Sumerian</a>: <span lang="sux-Xsux" style="font-size:125%;font-family:Akkadian" title="Classical Sumerian cuneiform">íÖ¥íÑÄ</span> <span title="Sumerian-language romanization"><i lang="sux-Latn">eme-gi</i></span> or <span lang="sux-Xsux" style="font-size:125%;font-family:Akkadian" title="Classical Sumerian cuneiform">íÖ¥íÇ†</span> <span title="Sumerian-language romanization"><i lang="sux-Latn">eme-ƒùir<sub>15</sub></i></span>, <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Akkadian</a>: <span lang="akk-Xsux" style="font-size:125%;font-family:Akkadian" title="Neo-Sumerian cuneiform">íãóíà®íäí</span> <span title="Akkadian-language romanization"><i lang="akk-Latn">≈°umeru</i></span>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> is the name given to the language spoken by the "Sumerians", the ancient non-<a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">Semitic</a>-speaking inhabitants of southern <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, by their successors the East Semitic-speaking <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_Empire" title="Akkadian Empire">Akkadians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ANE29_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-ANE29-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> The Sumerians referred to their land as <span title="Sumerian-language text"><i lang="sux-Latn">Kengir</i></span>, the 'Country of the noble lords' (<span lang="und-Xsux" style="font-size:125%;font-family:'Segoe UI Historic','Akkadian','Noto Sans Cuneiform','Noto Sans Sumero-Akkadian Cuneiform';" title="cuneiform text"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%F0%92%86%A0" title="wikt:íÜ†">íÜ†</a></span><span lang="und-Xsux" style="font-size:125%;font-family:'Segoe UI Historic','Akkadian','Noto Sans Cuneiform','Noto Sans Sumero-Akkadian Cuneiform';" title="cuneiform text"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%F0%92%82%97" title="wikt:íÇó">íÇó</a></span><span lang="und-Xsux" style="font-size:125%;font-family:'Segoe UI Historic','Akkadian','Noto Sans Cuneiform','Noto Sans Sumero-Akkadian Cuneiform';" title="cuneiform text"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%F0%92%84%80" title="wikt:íÑÄ">íÑÄ</a></span>, <span title="Sumerian-language text"><i lang="sux-Latn">k-en-gi(-r)</i></span>, lit. 'country' + 'lords' + 'noble') as seen in their inscriptions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ANE29_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-ANE29-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>The origin of the Sumerians is not known, but the people of Sumer referred to themselves as "Black Headed Ones" or "Black-Headed People"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ANE29_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-ANE29-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> (<span lang="und-Xsux" style="font-size:125%;font-family:'Segoe UI Historic','Akkadian','Noto Sans Cuneiform','Noto Sans Sumero-Akkadian Cuneiform';" title="cuneiform text"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%F0%92%8A%95" title="wikt:íäï">íäï</a></span> <span lang="und-Xsux" style="font-size:125%;font-family:'Segoe UI Historic','Akkadian','Noto Sans Cuneiform','Noto Sans Sumero-Akkadian Cuneiform';" title="cuneiform text"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%F0%92%88%AA" title="wikt:íà™">íà™</a></span>, <span title="Sumerian-language text"><i lang="sux-Latn">saƒù-g√≠g</i></span>, lit. 'head' + 'black', or <span lang="und-Xsux" style="font-size:125%;font-family:'Segoe UI Historic','Akkadian','Noto Sans Cuneiform','Noto Sans Sumero-Akkadian Cuneiform';" title="cuneiform text"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%F0%92%8A%95" title="wikt:íäï">íäï</a></span> <span lang="und-Xsux" style="font-size:125%;font-family:'Segoe UI Historic','Akkadian','Noto Sans Cuneiform','Noto Sans Sumero-Akkadian Cuneiform';" title="cuneiform text"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%F0%92%88%AA" title="wikt:íà™">íà™</a></span> <span lang="und-Xsux" style="font-size:125%;font-family:'Segoe UI Historic','Akkadian','Noto Sans Cuneiform','Noto Sans Sumero-Akkadian Cuneiform';" title="cuneiform text"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%F0%92%82%B5" title="wikt:íÇµ">íÇµ</a></span>, <span title="Sumerian-language text"><i lang="sux-Latn">saƒù-g√≠g-ga</i></span> phonetically <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/sa≈ã …°i …°a/</span>, lit. 'head' + 'black' + 'carry').<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAF_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-DAF-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PUSD_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-PUSD-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IMD_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-IMD-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GF_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-GF-4">[4]</a></sup> For example, the Sumerian king <a href="/wiki/Shulgi" title="Shulgi">Shulgi</a> described himself as "the king of the four quarters, the pastor of the black-headed people".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> The Akkadians also called the Sumerians 'black-headed people', or <span title="Akkadian-language text"><i lang="akk-Latn">·π£almat-qaqqadi</i></span>, in the Semitic Akkadian language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PUSD_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-PUSD-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IMD_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-IMD-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Akkadian word <span title="Akkadian-language text"><i lang="akk-Latn">≈†umer</i></span> may represent the geographical name in dialect, but the <a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">phonological</a> development leading to the Akkadian term <span title="Akkadian-language text"><i lang="akk-Latn">≈°umer√ª</i></span> is uncertain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nimrod_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nimrod-15">[15]</a></sup> Hebrew <span title="Hebrew-language text"><span lang="he"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1020748213">.mw-parser-output .script-hebrew,.mw-parser-output .script-Hebr{font-family:"SBL Hebrew","SBL BibLit","Taamey Ashkenaz","Taamey Frank CLM","Frank Ruehl CLM","Ezra SIL","Ezra SIL SR","Keter Aram Tsova","Taamey David CLM","Keter YG","Shofar","David CLM","Hadasim CLM","Simple CLM","Nachlieli",Cardo,Alef,"Noto Serif Hebrew","Noto Sans Hebrew","David Libre",David,"Times New Roman",Gisha,Arial,FreeSerif,FreeSans}</style><span class="script-hebrew" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 115%;">◊©÷¥◊Å◊†÷∞◊¢÷∏◊®</span>‚Äé</span></span> <span title="Hebrew-language text"><i lang="he-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Shinar" title="Shinar">≈†in øar</a></i></span>, <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_language" title="Egyptian language">Egyptian</a> <span title="Ancient Egyptian-language text"><i lang="egy-Latn">Sngr</i></span>, and <a href="/wiki/Hittite_language" title="Hittite language">Hittite</a> <span title="Hittite-language text"><i lang="hit-Latn">≈†anhar(a)</i></span>, all referring to southern Mesopotamia, could be western variants of <i>Sumer</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nimrod_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nimrod-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span></h2>
<p>Most historians have suggested that Sumer was first permanently settled between c. 5500 and 4000 BC by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/West_Asia" title="West Asia">West Asian</a> people who spoke the Sumerian language (pointing to the names of cities, rivers, basic occupations, etc., as evidence), a non-Semitic and non-<a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European</a> <a href="/wiki/Agglutinative_language" title="Agglutinative language">agglutinative</a> <a href="/wiki/Language_isolate" title="Language isolate">language isolate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SNK_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-SNK-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> In contrast to its <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Semitic-speaking_peoples" title="Ancient Semitic-speaking peoples">Semitic</a> neighbours, it was not an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inflected" title="Inflected">inflected</a> language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SNK_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-SNK-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blau_Monuments_(front).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="834" data-file-width="1660" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Blau_Monuments_%28front%29.jpg/330px-Blau_Monuments_%28front%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Blau_Monuments_%28front%29.jpg/495px-Blau_Monuments_%28front%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Blau_Monuments_%28front%29.jpg/660px-Blau_Monuments_%28front%29.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blau_Monuments_(front).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Blau_Monuments" title="Blau Monuments">Blau Monuments</a> combine <a href="/wiki/Cuneiform" title="Cuneiform">proto-cuneiform</a> characters and illustrations of early Sumerians, <a href="/wiki/Jemdet_Nasr_period" title="Jemdet Nasr period">Jemdet Nasr period</a>, 3100‚Äì2700 BC. British Museum.</div></div></div>
<p>Others have suggested that the Sumerians were a <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North African</a> people who migrated from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Green_Sahara" title="Green Sahara">Green Sahara</a> into the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> and were responsible for the spread of farming in the Middle East.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> However, with evidence strongly suggesting the first farmers originated from the <a href="/wiki/Fertile_Crescent" title="Fertile Crescent">Fertile Crescent</a>, this suggestion is often discarded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genomic_insights_into_the_origin_of_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Genomic_insights_into_the_origin_of-22">[22]</a></sup> Although not specifically discussing Sumerians, Lazaridis et al. 2016 have suggested <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_E-M215_(Y-DNA)" title="Haplogroup E-M215 (Y-DNA)">a partial North African origin</a> for some pre-Semitic cultures of the Middle East, particularly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natufians" title="Natufians">Natufians</a>, after testing the genomes of Natufian and <a href="/wiki/Pre-Pottery_Neolithic" title="Pre-Pottery Neolithic">Pre-Pottery Neolithic</a> culture-bearers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genomic_insights_into_the_origin_of_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Genomic_insights_into_the_origin_of-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Alternatively, a recent (2013) genetic analysis of four ancient Mesopotamian skeletal DNA samples suggests an association of the Sumerians with Indus Valley Civilization, possibly as a result of ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus-Mesopotamia_relations" title="Indus-Mesopotamia relations">Indus-Mesopotamia relations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBA_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-EBA-24">[24]</a></sup> According to some data, the Sumerians are associated with the <a href="/wiki/Hurrians" title="Hurrians">Hurrians</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urartians" title="Urartians">Urartians</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a> is considered their homeland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>A prehistoric people who lived in the region before the Sumerians have been termed the "<a href="/wiki/Proto-Euphratean_language" title="Proto-Euphratean language">Proto-Euphrateans</a>" or "<a href="/wiki/Ubaid_period" title="Ubaid period">Ubaidians</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-28">[28]</a></sup> and are theorized to have evolved from the <a href="/wiki/Samarra_culture" title="Samarra culture">Samarra culture</a> of northern Mesopotamia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> The Ubaidians, though never mentioned by the Sumerians themselves, are assumed by modern-day scholars to have been the first civilizing force in Sumer. They drained the marshes for <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>, developed trade, and established industries, including <a href="/wiki/Weaving" title="Weaving">weaving</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leather_crafting" title="Leather crafting">leatherwork</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metalworking" title="Metalworking">metalwork</a>, <a href="/wiki/Masonry" title="Masonry">masonry</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pottery" title="Pottery">pottery</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Enthroned_King_of_Ur.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2266" data-file-width="3229" decoding="async" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Enthroned_King_of_Ur.jpg/330px-Enthroned_King_of_Ur.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Enthroned_King_of_Ur.jpg/495px-Enthroned_King_of_Ur.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Enthroned_King_of_Ur.jpg/660px-Enthroned_King_of_Ur.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Enthroned_King_of_Ur.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Enthroned Sumerian king of <a href="/wiki/Ur" title="Ur">Ur</a>, possibly <a href="/wiki/Ur-Pabilsag" title="Ur-Pabilsag">Ur-Pabilsag</a>, with attendants. <a href="/wiki/Standard_of_Ur" title="Standard of Ur">Standard of Ur</a>, c. 2600 BC.</div></div></div>
<p>Some scholars contest the idea of a Proto-Euphratean language or one substrate language; they think the Sumerian language may originally have been that of the <a href="/wiki/Hunting" title="Hunting">hunting</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fishing" title="Fishing">fishing</a> peoples who lived in the marshland and the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Arabia" title="Eastern Arabia">Eastern Arabia littoral region</a> and were part of the Arabian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bifacial" title="Bifacial">bifacial</a> culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Reliable historical records begin much later; there are none in Sumer of any kind that have been dated before <a href="/wiki/Enmebaragesi" title="Enmebaragesi">Enmebaragesi</a> (<a href="/wiki/Early_Dynastic_Period_(Mesopotamia)" title="Early Dynastic Period (Mesopotamia)">Early Dynastic I</a>). <a href="/wiki/Juris_Zarins" title="Juris Zarins">Juris Zarins</a> believes the Sumerians lived along the coast of <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Arabia" title="Eastern Arabia">Eastern Arabia</a>, today's Persian Gulf region, before it was flooded at the end of <a href="/wiki/Last_Glacial_Period" title="Last Glacial Period">the Ice Age</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sumerian civilization took form in the <a href="/wiki/Uruk_period" title="Uruk period">Uruk period</a> (4th millennium BC), continuing into the <a href="/wiki/Jemdet_Nasr_period" title="Jemdet Nasr period">Jemdet Nasr</a> and <a href="/wiki/Early_Dynastic_Period_(Mesopotamia)" title="Early Dynastic Period (Mesopotamia)">Early Dynastic</a> periods.
</p><p>The Sumerians progressively lost control to Semitic states from the northwest. Sumer was conquered by the Semitic-speaking kings of the Akkadian Empire around 2270 BC (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Short_chronology" title="Short chronology">short chronology</a>), but Sumerian continued as a <a href="/wiki/Sacred_language" title="Sacred language">sacred language</a>. Native Sumerian rule re-emerged for about a century in the <a href="/wiki/Third_Dynasty_of_Ur" title="Third Dynasty of Ur">Third Dynasty of Ur</a> at approximately 2100‚Äì2000 BC, but the Akkadian language also remained in use for some time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leick,_Gwendolyn_2003_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leick,_Gwendolyn_2003-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Sumerian city of <a href="/wiki/Eridu" title="Eridu">Eridu</a>, on the coast of the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a>, is considered to have been one of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_oldest_continuously_inhabited_cities" title="List of oldest continuously inhabited cities">oldest cities</a>, where three separate cultures may have fused: that of peasant Ubaidian farmers, living in mud-brick huts and practicing irrigation; that of mobile nomadic Semitic pastoralists living in black tents and following herds of sheep and goats; and that of fisher folk, living in reed huts in the marshlands, who may have been the ancestors of the Sumerians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leick,_Gwendolyn_2003_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Leick,_Gwendolyn_2003-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="City-states_in_Mesopotamia">City-states in Mesopotamia</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_of_the_ancient_Near_East" title="List of cities of the ancient Near East">List of cities of the ancient Near East</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Mesopotamia" title="Geography of Mesopotamia">Geography of Mesopotamia</a></div>
<p>In the late 4th millennium BC, Sumer was divided into many independent <a href="/wiki/City-state" title="City-state">city-states</a>, which were divided by canals and boundary stones. Each was centered on a temple dedicated to the particular patron god or goddess of the city and ruled over by a priestly governor (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ens%C3%AD" title="Ens√≠">ensi</a>) or by a king (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LUGAL" title="LUGAL">lugal</a>) who was intimately tied to the city's religious rites.
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:292px;max-width:292px"><div class="trow"><div class="theader">Anu ziggurat and White Temple</div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:290px;max-width:290px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:170px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:White_Temple_ziggurat_in_Uruk.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1158" data-file-width="1952" decoding="async" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/White_Temple_ziggurat_in_Uruk.jpg/288px-White_Temple_ziggurat_in_Uruk.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/White_Temple_ziggurat_in_Uruk.jpg/432px-White_Temple_ziggurat_in_Uruk.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/White_Temple_ziggurat_in_Uruk.jpg/576px-White_Temple_ziggurat_in_Uruk.jpg 2x" width="288"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:290px;max-width:290px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:206px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_White_Temple_%27E_at_Uruk,_3500-3000_BCE.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="847" data-file-width="1184" decoding="async" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/The_White_Temple_%27E_at_Uruk%2C_3500-3000_BCE.jpg/288px-The_White_Temple_%27E_at_Uruk%2C_3500-3000_BCE.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/The_White_Temple_%27E_at_Uruk%2C_3500-3000_BCE.jpg/432px-The_White_Temple_%27E_at_Uruk%2C_3500-3000_BCE.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/The_White_Temple_%27E_at_Uruk%2C_3500-3000_BCE.jpg/576px-The_White_Temple_%27E_at_Uruk%2C_3500-3000_BCE.jpg 2x" width="288"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Anu ziggurat and White Temple  at Uruk. The original pyramidal structure, the "Anu Ziggurat" dates to around 4000 BC, and the White Temple was built on top of it c. 3500 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The design of the ziggurat was probably a precursor to that of the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_pyramids" title="Egyptian pyramids">Egyptian pyramids</a>, the earliest of which dates to c. 2600 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></div></div></div></div>
<p>The five "first" cities, said to have exercised <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sumerian_king_list" title="Sumerian king list">pre-dynastic</a> kingship "before the flood":
</p>
<ol><li><a href="/wiki/Eridu" title="Eridu">Eridu</a> (<i>Tell Abu Shahrain</i>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bad-tibira" title="Bad-tibira">Bad-tibira</a> (probably <i>Tell al-Madain)</i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Larak_(Sumer)" title="Larak (Sumer)">Larak</a> <sup>1</sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sippar" title="Sippar">Sippar</a> (<i>Tell Abu Habbah</i>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Shuruppak" title="Shuruppak">Shuruppak</a> (<i>Tell Fara</i>)</li></ol>
<p>Other principal cities:
</p>
<div><ol start="6"><li><a href="/wiki/Uruk" title="Uruk">Uruk</a> (<i>Warka</i>)</li><li><a href="/wiki/Kish_(Sumer)" title="Kish (Sumer)">Kish</a> (<i>Tell Uheimir and Ingharra</i>)</li><li><a href="/wiki/Ur" title="Ur">Ur</a> (<i>Tell al-Muqayyar</i>)</li><li><a href="/wiki/Nippur" title="Nippur">Nippur</a> (<i>Afak</i>)</li><li><a href="/wiki/Lagash" title="Lagash">Lagash</a> (<i>Tell al-Hiba</i>)</li><li><a href="/wiki/Girsu" title="Girsu">Girsu</a> (<i>Tello or Telloh</i>)</li><li><a href="/wiki/Umma" title="Umma">Umma</a> (<i>Tell Jokha</i>)</li><li><a href="/wiki/Hamazi" title="Hamazi">Hamazi</a> <sup>1</sup></li><li><a href="/wiki/Adab_(city)" title="Adab (city)">Adab</a> (<i>Tell Bismaya</i>)</li><li><a href="/wiki/Mari,_Syria" title="Mari, Syria">Mari</a> (<i>Tell Hariri</i>) <sup>2</sup></li><li><a href="/wiki/Akshak" title="Akshak">Akshak</a> <sup>1</sup></li><li><a href="/wiki/Akkad_(city)" title="Akkad (city)">Akkad</a> <sup>1</sup></li><li><a href="/wiki/Isin" title="Isin">Isin</a> (<i>Ishan al-Bahriyat</i>)</li><li><a href="/wiki/Larsa" title="Larsa">Larsa</a> (<i>Tell as-Senkereh</i>)</li></ol></div>
<div class="plainlist">
<ul><li><span style="font-size:85%;">(<sup>1</sup>location uncertain)</span></li>
<li><span style="font-size:85%;">(<sup>2</sup>an outlying city in northern Mesopotamia)</span></li></ul>
</div>
<p>Minor cities (from south to north):
</p>
<ol><li><a href="/wiki/Kuara_(Sumer)" title="Kuara (Sumer)">Kuara</a> (<i>Tell al-Lahm</i>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Zabala_(Sumer)" title="Zabala (Sumer)">Zabala</a> (<i>Tell Ibzeikh</i>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kisurra" title="Kisurra">Kisurra</a> (<i>Tell Abu Hatab</i>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Marad" title="Marad">Marad</a> (<i>Tell Wannat es-Sadum</i>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dilbat" title="Dilbat">Dilbat</a> (<i>Tell ed-Duleim</i>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Borsippa" title="Borsippa">Borsippa</a> (<i>Birs Nimrud</i>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kutha" title="Kutha">Kutha</a> (<i>Tell Ibrahim</i>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Der_(Sumer)" title="Der (Sumer)">Der</a> (<i>al-Badra</i>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Eshnunna" title="Eshnunna">Eshnunna</a> (<i>Tell Asmar</i>)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nagar,_Syria" title="Nagar, Syria">Nagar</a> (<i>Tell Brak</i>) <sup>2</sup></li></ol>
<p><small>(<sup>2</sup>an outlying city in northern Mesopotamia)</small>
</p><p>Apart from Mari, which lies full 330 kilometres (205 miles) north-west of Agade, but which is credited in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sumerian_king_list" title="Sumerian king list">king list</a> as having "exercised kingship" in the Early Dynastic II period, and Nagar, an outpost, these cities are all in the Euphrates-Tigris alluvial plain, south of <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a> in what are now the <a href="/wiki/Babil_Governorate" title="Babil Governorate">BƒÅbil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diyala_Governorate" title="Diyala Governorate">Diyala</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wasit_Governorate" title="Wasit Governorate">WƒÅsit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dhi_Qar_Governorate" title="Dhi Qar Governorate">Dhi Qar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Basra_Governorate" title="Basra Governorate">Basra</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al_Muthanna_Governorate" title="Al Muthanna Governorate">Al-MuthannƒÅ</a> and <a href="/wiki/Al-Q%C4%81disiyyah_Governorate" title="Al-QƒÅdisiyyah Governorate">Al-QƒÅdisiyyah</a> governorates of <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Sumer" title="History of Sumer">History of Sumer</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Portrait_of_a_Sumerian_prisoner_on_a_victory_stele_of_Sargon_of_Akkad.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1395" data-file-width="1169" decoding="async" height="263" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Portrait_of_a_Sumerian_prisoner_on_a_victory_stele_of_Sargon_of_Akkad.jpg/220px-Portrait_of_a_Sumerian_prisoner_on_a_victory_stele_of_Sargon_of_Akkad.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Portrait_of_a_Sumerian_prisoner_on_a_victory_stele_of_Sargon_of_Akkad.jpg/330px-Portrait_of_a_Sumerian_prisoner_on_a_victory_stele_of_Sargon_of_Akkad.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Portrait_of_a_Sumerian_prisoner_on_a_victory_stele_of_Sargon_of_Akkad.jpg/440px-Portrait_of_a_Sumerian_prisoner_on_a_victory_stele_of_Sargon_of_Akkad.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Portrait_of_a_Sumerian_prisoner_on_a_victory_stele_of_Sargon_of_Akkad.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of a Sumerian prisoner on a victory stele of <a href="/wiki/Sargon_of_Akkad" title="Sargon of Akkad">Sargon of Akkad</a>, c. 2300 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ArchaeologyofElam_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-ArchaeologyofElam-39">[39]</a></sup> The hairstyle of the prisoners (curly hair on top and short hair on the sides) is characteristic of Sumerians, as also seen on the <a href="/wiki/Standard_of_Ur" title="Standard of Ur">Standard of Ur</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TwoSteles_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-TwoSteles-40">[40]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louvre_Museum" title="Louvre Museum">Louvre Museum</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The Sumerian city-states rose to power during the prehistoric Ubaid and Uruk periods. Sumerian written history reaches back to the 27th century BC and before, but the historical record remains obscure until the Early Dynastic III period, c. 23rd century BC, when a now deciphered syllabary writing system was developed, which has allowed archaeologists to read contemporary records and inscriptions. The <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_Empire" title="Akkadian Empire">Akkadian Empire</a> was the first state that successfully united larger parts of Mesopotamia in the 23rd century BC. After the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gutian_period" title="Gutian period">Gutian period</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Third_Dynasty_of_Ur" title="Third Dynasty of Ur">Ur III kingdom</a> similarly united parts of northern and southern Mesopotamia. It ended in the face of <a href="/wiki/Amorites" title="Amorites">Amorite</a> incursions at the beginning of the second millennium BC. The Amorite "dynasty of <a href="/wiki/Isin" title="Isin">Isin</a>" persisted until c. 1700 BC, when Mesopotamia was united under <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonian</a> rule.
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Ubaid_period" title="Ubaid period">Ubaid period</a>: 6500‚Äì4100 BC (Pottery <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> to <a href="/wiki/Chalcolithic" title="Chalcolithic">Chalcolithic</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Uruk_period" title="Uruk period">Uruk period</a>: 4100‚Äì2900 BC (Late <a href="/wiki/Chalcolithic" title="Chalcolithic">Chalcolithic</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Bronze_Age" title="Early Bronze Age">Early Bronze Age</a> I)
<ul><li>Uruk XIV‚ÄìV: 4100‚Äì3300 BC</li>
<li>Uruk IV period: 3300‚Äì3100 BC</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jemdet_Nasr_period" title="Jemdet Nasr period">Jemdet Nasr period</a> (Uruk III): 3100‚Äì2900 BC</li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Early_Dynastic_Period_(Mesopotamia)" title="Early Dynastic Period (Mesopotamia)">Early Dynastic period</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Bronze_Age" title="Early Bronze Age">Early Bronze Age</a> II‚ÄìIV)
<ul><li>Early Dynastic I period: 2900‚Äì2800 BC</li>
<li>Early Dynastic II period: 2800‚Äì2600 BC (<a href="/wiki/Gilgamesh" title="Gilgamesh">Gilgamesh</a>)</li>
<li>Early Dynastic IIIa period: 2600‚Äì2500 BC</li>
<li>Early Dynastic IIIb period: c. 2500‚Äì2334 BC</li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Akkadian_Empire" title="Akkadian Empire">Akkadian Empire</a> period: c. 2334‚Äì2218 BC (<a href="/wiki/Sargon_of_Akkad" title="Sargon of Akkad">Sargon</a>)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gutian_period" title="Gutian period">Gutian period</a>: c. 2218‚Äì2047 BC (Early Bronze Age IV)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ur_III_period" title="Ur III period">Ur III period</a>: c. 2047‚Äì1940 BC</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ubaid_period">Ubaid period</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ubaid_period" title="Ubaid period">Ubaid period</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Frieze-group-3-example1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1830" data-file-width="1815" decoding="async" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Frieze-group-3-example1.jpg/220px-Frieze-group-3-example1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Frieze-group-3-example1.jpg/330px-Frieze-group-3-example1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Frieze-group-3-example1.jpg/440px-Frieze-group-3-example1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Frieze-group-3-example1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pottery jar from Late Ubaid Period</div></div></div>
<p>The Ubaid period is marked by a distinctive style of fine quality painted pottery which spread throughout Mesopotamia and the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a>. The oldest evidence for occupation comes from <a href="/wiki/Tell_el-%27Oueili" title="Tell el-'Oueili">Tell el-'Oueili</a>, but, given that environmental conditions in southern Mesopotamia were favourable to human occupation well before the Ubaid period, it is likely that older sites exist but have not yet been found. It appears that this culture was derived from the <a href="/wiki/Samarra" title="Samarra">Samarran</a> culture from northern Mesopotamia. It is not known whether or not these were the actual Sumerians who are identified with the later Uruk culture. The story of the passing of the gifts of civilization (<a href="/wiki/Me_(mythology)" title="Me (mythology)"><i>me</i></a>) to <a href="/wiki/Inanna" title="Inanna">Inanna</a>, goddess of Uruk and of love and war, by <a href="/wiki/Enki" title="Enki">Enki</a>, god of wisdom and chief god of Eridu, may reflect the transition from Eridu to Uruk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WolksteinKramer1983_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-WolksteinKramer1983-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Uruk_period">Uruk period</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Uruk_period" title="Uruk period">Uruk period</a></div>
<p>The archaeological transition from the Ubaid period to the Uruk period is marked by a gradual shift from painted pottery domestically produced on a slow <a href="/wiki/Potter%27s_wheel" title="Potter's wheel">wheel</a> to a great variety of unpainted pottery mass-produced by specialists on fast wheels. The Uruk period is a continuation and an outgrowth of Ubaid with pottery being the main visible change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:242px;max-width:242px"><div class="trow"><div class="theader">Uruk King-priest feeding the sacred herd</div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:240px;max-width:240px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:96px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yale_University._Uruk_period_priest-king.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="841" data-file-width="2071" decoding="async" height="97" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Yale_University._Uruk_period_priest-king.jpg/238px-Yale_University._Uruk_period_priest-king.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Yale_University._Uruk_period_priest-king.jpg/357px-Yale_University._Uruk_period_priest-king.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Yale_University._Uruk_period_priest-king.jpg/476px-Yale_University._Uruk_period_priest-king.jpg 2x" width="238"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">The king-priest and his acolyte feeding the sacred herd. Uruk period, c. 3200 BC</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:240px;max-width:240px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:187px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uruk_King_priest_feeding_the_sacred_herd.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="746" data-file-width="949" decoding="async" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Uruk_King_priest_feeding_the_sacred_herd.jpg/238px-Uruk_King_priest_feeding_the_sacred_herd.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Uruk_King_priest_feeding_the_sacred_herd.jpg/357px-Uruk_King_priest_feeding_the_sacred_herd.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Uruk_King_priest_feeding_the_sacred_herd.jpg/476px-Uruk_King_priest_feeding_the_sacred_herd.jpg 2x" width="238"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center"><a href="/wiki/Cylinder_seal" title="Cylinder seal">Cylinder seal</a> of the Uruk period and its impression, c. 3100 BC - <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louvre_Museum" title="Louvre Museum">Louvre Museum</a></div></div></div></div></div>
<p>By the time of the Uruk period (c. 4100‚Äì2900 BC calibrated), the volume of trade goods transported along the canals and rivers of southern Mesopotamia facilitated the rise of many large, <a href="/wiki/Social_stratification" title="Social stratification">stratified</a>, temple-centered cities (with populations of over 10,000 people) where centralized administrations employed specialized workers. It is fairly certain that it was during the Uruk period that Sumerian cities began to make use of <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_antiquity" title="Slavery in antiquity">slave labour</a> captured from the hill country, and there is ample evidence for captured slaves as workers in the earliest texts. Artifacts, and even colonies of this Uruk civilization have been found over a wide area‚Äîfrom the <a href="/wiki/Taurus_Mountains" title="Taurus Mountains">Taurus Mountains</a> in <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, to the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a> in the west, and as far east as western <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Algaze,_Guillermo_2005_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Algaze,_Guillermo_2005-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 2‚Äì3">:‚Ää2‚Äì3‚Ää</span></sup>
</p><p>The Uruk period civilization, exported by Sumerian traders and colonists (like that found at <a href="/wiki/Tell_Brak" title="Tell Brak">Tell Brak</a>), had an effect on all surrounding peoples, who gradually evolved their own comparable, competing economies and cultures. The cities of Sumer could not maintain remote, long-distance colonies by military force.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Algaze,_Guillermo_2005_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Algaze,_Guillermo_2005-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (August 2021)">page¬†needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Sumerian cities during the Uruk period were probably <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theocratic" title="Theocratic">theocratic</a> and were most likely headed by a priest-king (<i>ensi</i>), assisted by a council of elders, including both men and women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacobsen_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobsen-45">[45]</a></sup> It is quite possible that the later Sumerian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pantheon_(gods)" title="Pantheon (gods)">pantheon</a> was modeled upon this political structure. There was little evidence of organized warfare or professional soldiers during the Uruk period, and towns were generally unwalled. During this period Uruk became the most urbanized city in the world, surpassing for the first time 50,000 inhabitants.
</p><p>The ancient Sumerian king list includes the early dynasties of several prominent cities from this period. The first set of names on the list is of kings said to have reigned before a major flood occurred. These early names may be fictional, and include some legendary and mythological figures, such as <a href="/wiki/Alulim" title="Alulim">Alulim</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dumuzid,_the_Shepherd" title="Dumuzid, the Shepherd">Dumizid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacobsen_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobsen-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>The end of the Uruk period coincided with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Piora_oscillation" title="Piora oscillation">Piora oscillation</a>, a dry period from c. 3200‚Äì2900 BC that marked the end of a long wetter, warmer climate period from about 9,000 to 5,000 years ago, called the <a href="/wiki/Holocene_climatic_optimum" title="Holocene climatic optimum">Holocene climatic optimum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Dynastic_Period">Early Dynastic Period</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Early_Dynastic_Period_(Mesopotamia)" title="Early Dynastic Period (Mesopotamia)">Early Dynastic Period (Mesopotamia)</a> and <a href="/wiki/First_Dynasty_of_Ur" title="First Dynasty of Ur">First Dynasty of Ur</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Meskalamdug_helmet_British_Museum_electrotype_copy_original_is_in_the_Iraq_Museum,_Bagdad.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2995" data-file-width="3207" decoding="async" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Meskalamdug_helmet_British_Museum_electrotype_copy_original_is_in_the_Iraq_Museum%2C_Bagdad.jpg/220px-Meskalamdug_helmet_British_Museum_electrotype_copy_original_is_in_the_Iraq_Museum%2C_Bagdad.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Meskalamdug_helmet_British_Museum_electrotype_copy_original_is_in_the_Iraq_Museum%2C_Bagdad.jpg/330px-Meskalamdug_helmet_British_Museum_electrotype_copy_original_is_in_the_Iraq_Museum%2C_Bagdad.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Meskalamdug_helmet_British_Museum_electrotype_copy_original_is_in_the_Iraq_Museum%2C_Bagdad.jpg/440px-Meskalamdug_helmet_British_Museum_electrotype_copy_original_is_in_the_Iraq_Museum%2C_Bagdad.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Meskalamdug_helmet_British_Museum_electrotype_copy_original_is_in_the_Iraq_Museum,_Bagdad.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Golden helmet of <a href="/wiki/Meskalamdug" title="Meskalamdug">Meskalamdug</a>, possible founder of the <a href="/wiki/First_Dynasty_of_Ur" title="First Dynasty of Ur">First Dynasty of Ur</a>, 26th century BC</div></div></div>
<p>The dynastic period begins c. 2900 BC and was associated with a shift from the temple establishment headed by council of elders led by a priestly "En" (a male figure when it was a temple for a goddess, or a female figure when headed by a male god)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> towards a more secular Lugal (Lu = man, Gal = great) and includes such legendary patriarchal figures as <a href="/wiki/Dumuzid" title="Dumuzid">Dumuzid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lugalbanda" title="Lugalbanda">Lugalbanda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gilgamesh" title="Gilgamesh">Gilgamesh</a>‚Äîwho reigned shortly before the historic record opens c. 2900 BC, when the now deciphered syllabic writing started to develop from the early pictograms. The center of Sumerian culture remained in southern Mesopotamia, even though rulers soon began expanding into neighboring areas, and neighboring Semitic groups adopted much of Sumerian culture for their own.
</p><p>The earliest dynastic king on the Sumerian king list whose name is known from any other legendary source is <a href="/wiki/Etana" title="Etana">Etana</a>, 13th king of the first dynasty of <a href="/wiki/Kish_(Sumer)" title="Kish (Sumer)">Kish</a>. The earliest king authenticated through archaeological evidence is <a href="/wiki/Enmebaragesi" title="Enmebaragesi">Enmebaragesi</a> of Kish (Early Dynastic I), whose name is also mentioned in the <i><a href="/wiki/Epic_of_Gilgamesh" title="Epic of Gilgamesh">Epic of Gilgamesh</a></i>‚Äîleading to the suggestion that Gilgamesh himself might have been a historical king of Uruk. As the <i>Epic of Gilgamesh</i> shows, this period was associated with increased war. Cities became walled, and increased in size as undefended villages in southern Mesopotamia disappeared. (Both Enmerkar and Gilgamesh are credited with having built the walls of Uruk.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="1st_Dynasty_of_Lagash">1st Dynasty of Lagash</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stele_of_Vultures_detail_02.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1100" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="202" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Stele_of_Vultures_detail_02.jpg/220px-Stele_of_Vultures_detail_02.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Stele_of_Vultures_detail_02.jpg/330px-Stele_of_Vultures_detail_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Stele_of_Vultures_detail_02.jpg/440px-Stele_of_Vultures_detail_02.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stele_of_Vultures_detail_02.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fragment of <a href="/wiki/Eannatum" title="Eannatum">Eannatum</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Stele_of_the_Vultures" title="Stele of the Vultures">Stele of the Vultures</a></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lagash" title="Lagash">Lagash</a></div>
<p>The dynasty of Lagash (c. 2500‚Äì2270 BC), though omitted from the king list, is well attested through several important monuments and many archaeological finds.
</p><p>Although short-lived, one of the first empires known to history was that of <a href="/wiki/Eannatum" title="Eannatum">Eannatum</a> of Lagash, who annexed practically all of Sumer, including Kish, Uruk, <a href="/wiki/Ur" title="Ur">Ur</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Larsa" title="Larsa">Larsa</a>, and reduced to tribute the city-state of <a href="/wiki/Umma" title="Umma">Umma</a>, arch-rival of Lagash. In addition, his realm extended to parts of <a href="/wiki/Elam" title="Elam">Elam</a> and along the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a>. He seems to have used terror as a matter of policy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-roux1993_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-roux1993-49">[49]</a></sup> Eannatum's <a href="/wiki/Stele_of_the_Vultures" title="Stele of the Vultures">Stele of the Vultures</a> depicts vultures pecking at the severed heads and other body parts of his enemies. His empire collapsed shortly after his death.
</p><p>Later, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lugal-Zage-Si" title="Lugal-Zage-Si">Lugal-Zage-Si</a>, the priest-king of Umma, overthrew the primacy of the Lagash dynasty in the area, then conquered Uruk, making it his capital, and claimed an empire extending from the Persian Gulf to the Mediterranean. He was the last ethnically Sumerian king before <a href="/wiki/Sargon_of_Akkad" title="Sargon of Akkad">Sargon of Akkad</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leick,_Gwendolyn_2003_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-Leick,_Gwendolyn_2003-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Akkadian_Empire">Akkadian Empire</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_Empire" title="Akkadian Empire">Akkadian Empire</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Prisoners_on_the_victory_stele_of_an_Akkadian_king_circa_2300_BCE_Louvre_Museum_Sb_3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1276" data-file-width="946" decoding="async" height="297" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Prisoners_on_the_victory_stele_of_an_Akkadian_king_circa_2300_BCE_Louvre_Museum_Sb_3.jpg/220px-Prisoners_on_the_victory_stele_of_an_Akkadian_king_circa_2300_BCE_Louvre_Museum_Sb_3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Prisoners_on_the_victory_stele_of_an_Akkadian_king_circa_2300_BCE_Louvre_Museum_Sb_3.jpg/330px-Prisoners_on_the_victory_stele_of_an_Akkadian_king_circa_2300_BCE_Louvre_Museum_Sb_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Prisoners_on_the_victory_stele_of_an_Akkadian_king_circa_2300_BCE_Louvre_Museum_Sb_3.jpg/440px-Prisoners_on_the_victory_stele_of_an_Akkadian_king_circa_2300_BCE_Louvre_Museum_Sb_3.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Prisoners_on_the_victory_stele_of_an_Akkadian_king_circa_2300_BCE_Louvre_Museum_Sb_3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sumerian prisoners on a victory stele of the Akkadian king <a href="/wiki/Sargon_of_Akkad" title="Sargon of Akkad">Sargon</a>, c. 2300 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ArchaeologyofElam_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-ArchaeologyofElam-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TwoSteles_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-TwoSteles-40">[40]</a></sup> Louvre Museum.</div></div></div>
<p>The Akkadian Empire dates to c. 2234‚Äì2154 BC (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Middle_chronology" title="Middle chronology">middle chronology</a>). The <a href="/wiki/East_Semitic_languages" title="East Semitic languages">Eastern Semitic</a> <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Akkadian language</a> is first attested in proper names of the kings of Kish c. 2800 BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-roux1993_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-roux1993-49">[49]</a></sup> preserved in later king lists. There are texts written entirely in Old Akkadian dating from c. 2500 BC. Use of Old Akkadian was at its peak during the rule of <a href="/wiki/Sargon_of_Akkad" title="Sargon of Akkad">Sargon the Great</a> (c. 2334‚Äì2279 BC), but even then most administrative tablets continued to be written in Sumerian, the language used by the scribes. Gelb and Westenholz differentiate three stages of Old Akkadian: that of the pre-Sargonic era, that of the Akkadian empire, and that of the Ur III period that followed it. Akkadian and Sumerian coexisted as vernacular languages for about one thousand years, but by around 1800 BC, Sumerian was becoming more of a literary language familiar mainly only to scholars and scribes. <a href="/wiki/Thorkild_Jacobsen" title="Thorkild Jacobsen">Thorkild Jacobsen</a> has argued that there is little break in historical continuity between the pre- and post-Sargon periods, and that too much emphasis has been placed on the perception of a "Semitic vs. Sumerian" conflict.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> However, it is certain that Akkadian was also briefly imposed on neighboring parts of Elam that were previously conquered, by Sargon.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gutian_period">Gutian period</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gutian_dynasty_of_Sumer" title="Gutian dynasty of Sumer">Gutian dynasty of Sumer</a></div>
<p>c. 2193‚Äì2119 BC (middle chronology)
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="2nd_Dynasty_of_Lagash">2nd Dynasty of Lagash</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gudea_of_Lagash_Girsu.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3900" data-file-width="2600" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Gudea_of_Lagash_Girsu.jpg/220px-Gudea_of_Lagash_Girsu.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Gudea_of_Lagash_Girsu.jpg/330px-Gudea_of_Lagash_Girsu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Gudea_of_Lagash_Girsu.jpg/440px-Gudea_of_Lagash_Girsu.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gudea_of_Lagash_Girsu.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Gudea" title="Gudea">Gudea</a> of <a href="/wiki/Lagash" title="Lagash">Lagash</a>, the Sumerian ruler who was famous for his numerous portrait sculptures that have been recovered.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ur-Ningirsu_ruler_of_Lagash_portrait_circa_2110_BCE.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2856" data-file-width="1914" decoding="async" height="328" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Ur-Ningirsu_ruler_of_Lagash_portrait_circa_2110_BCE.jpg/220px-Ur-Ningirsu_ruler_of_Lagash_portrait_circa_2110_BCE.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Ur-Ningirsu_ruler_of_Lagash_portrait_circa_2110_BCE.jpg/330px-Ur-Ningirsu_ruler_of_Lagash_portrait_circa_2110_BCE.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Ur-Ningirsu_ruler_of_Lagash_portrait_circa_2110_BCE.jpg/440px-Ur-Ningirsu_ruler_of_Lagash_portrait_circa_2110_BCE.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ur-Ningirsu_ruler_of_Lagash_portrait_circa_2110_BCE.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of <a href="/wiki/Ur-Ningirsu" title="Ur-Ningirsu">Ur-Ningirsu</a>, son of Gudea, c. 2100 BC. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louvre_Museum" title="Louvre Museum">Louvre Museum</a>.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lagash" title="Lagash">Lagash</a></div>
<p>c. 2200‚Äì2110 BC (middle chronology)
</p><p>Following the downfall of the Akkadian Empire at the hands of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gutian_dynasty_of_Sumer" title="Gutian dynasty of Sumer">Gutians</a>, another native Sumerian ruler, <a href="/wiki/Gudea" title="Gudea">Gudea</a> of Lagash, rose to local prominence and continued the practices of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_kings_of_Akkad#Sargonic_dynasty_(c._2334_‚Äì_2193_BC)" title="List of kings of Akkad">Sargonic kings</a>' claims to divinity.
</p><p>The previous Lagash dynasty, Gudea and his descendants also promoted artistic development and left a large number of archaeological artifacts.
</p>
<h3><span id=".22Neo-Sumerian.22_Ur_III_period"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='"Neo-Sumerian"_Ur_III_period'>"Neo-Sumerian" Ur III period</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ziggurat_of_ur.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="666" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Ziggurat_of_ur.jpg/220px-Ziggurat_of_ur.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Ziggurat_of_ur.jpg/330px-Ziggurat_of_ur.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Ziggurat_of_ur.jpg/440px-Ziggurat_of_ur.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ziggurat_of_ur.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Ziggurat_of_Ur" title="Great Ziggurat of Ur">Great Ziggurat of Ur</a>, c. 2100 BC, near <a href="/wiki/Nasiriyah" title="Nasiriyah">Nasiriyah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Third_Dynasty_of_Ur" title="Third Dynasty of Ur">Third Dynasty of Ur</a></div>
<p>Later, the Third Dynasty of Ur under <a href="/wiki/Ur-Nammu" title="Ur-Nammu">Ur-Nammu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shulgi" title="Shulgi">Shulgi</a> (c. 2112‚Äì2004 BC, middle chronology), whose power extended as far as southern <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyria</a>, has been erroneously called a "Sumerian renaissance" in the past.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Already, however, the region was becoming more Semitic than Sumerian, with the resurgence of the Akkadian-speaking Semites in Assyria and elsewhere, and the influx of waves of Semitic Martu (<a href="/wiki/Amorites" title="Amorites">Amorites</a>), who were to found several competing local powers in the south, including <a href="/wiki/Isin" title="Isin">Isin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Larsa" title="Larsa">Larsa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eshnunna" title="Eshnunna">Eshnunna</a> and later, Babylonia. The last of these eventually came to briefly dominate the south of Mesopotamia as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Babylonian_Empire" title="Babylonian Empire">Babylonian Empire</a>, just as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Assyrian_Empire" title="Old Assyrian Empire">Old Assyrian Empire</a> had already done in the north from the late 21st century BC. The Sumerian language continued as a sacerdotal language taught in schools in Babylonia and Assyria, much as Latin was used in the Medieval period, for as long as cuneiform was used.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fall_and_transmission">Fall and transmission</span></h3>
<p>This period is generally taken to coincide with a major shift in population from southern Mesopotamia toward the north. Ecologically, the agricultural productivity of the Sumerian lands was being compromised as a result of rising salinity. <a href="/wiki/Soil_salinity" title="Soil salinity">Soil salinity</a> in this region had been long recognized as a major problem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Poorly drained irrigated soils, in an arid climate with high levels of evaporation, led to the buildup of dissolved salts in the soil, eventually reducing agricultural yields severely. During the Akkadian and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ur_III" title="Ur III">Ur III</a> phases, there was a shift from the cultivation of <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a> to the more salt-tolerant <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">barley</a>, but this was insufficient, and during the period from 2100 BC to 1700 BC, it is estimated that the population in this area declined by nearly three-fifths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> This greatly upset the balance of power within the region, weakening the areas where Sumerian was spoken, and comparatively strengthening those where Akkadian was the major language. Henceforth, Sumerian would remain only a <a href="/wiki/Literary_language" title="Literary language">literary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sacred_language" title="Sacred language">liturgical</a> language, similar to the position occupied by <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> in <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">medieval</a> Europe.
</p><p>Following an Elamite invasion and sack of Ur during the rule of <a href="/wiki/Ibbi-Sin" title="Ibbi-Sin">Ibbi-Sin</a> (c. 2028‚Äì2004 BC),<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Doesn't cite any evidence of sack of Ur? (October 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Sumer came under Amorite rule (taken to introduce the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Middle_Bronze_Age" title="Middle Bronze Age">Middle Bronze Age</a>). The independent Amorite states of the 20th to 18th centuries are summarized as the "<a href="/wiki/Dynasty_of_Isin" title="Dynasty of Isin">Dynasty of Isin</a>" in the Sumerian king list, ending with the rise of Babylonia under <a href="/wiki/Hammurabi" title="Hammurabi">Hammurabi</a> c. 1800 BC.
</p><p>Later rulers who dominated Assyria and Babylonia occasionally assumed the old Sargonic title "King of Sumer and Akkad", such as <a href="/wiki/Tukulti-Ninurta_I" title="Tukulti-Ninurta I">Tukulti-Ninurta I</a> of Assyria after c. 1225 BC.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Population">Population</span></h2>
<p>Uruk, one of Sumer's largest cities, has been estimated to have had a population of 50,000‚Äì80,000 at its height;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> given the other cities in Sumer, and the large agricultural population, a rough estimate for Sumer's population might be 0.8 million to 1.5 million. The <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">world population</a> at this time has been estimated at about 27 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Sumerians spoke a <a href="/wiki/Language_isolate" title="Language isolate">language isolate</a>, but a number of linguists have claimed to be able to detect a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Substrate_language" title="Substrate language">substrate language</a> of unknown classification beneath Sumerian because names of some of Sumer's major cities are not Sumerian, revealing influences of earlier inhabitants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nemet-Nejat1998_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nemet-Nejat1998-56">[56]</a></sup> However, the <a href="/wiki/Archaeological_record" title="Archaeological record">archaeological record</a> shows clear uninterrupted cultural continuity from the time of the early <a href="/wiki/Ubaid_period" title="Ubaid period">Ubaid period</a> (5300‚Äì4700 BC <a href="/wiki/Radiocarbon_dating" title="Radiocarbon dating">C-14</a>) settlements in southern Mesopotamia. The Sumerian people who settled here farmed the lands in this region that were made fertile by silt deposited by the <a href="/wiki/Tigris" title="Tigris">Tigris</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a>.
</p><p>Some archaeologists have speculated that the original speakers of ancient Sumerian may have been farmers, who moved down from the north of Mesopotamia after perfecting irrigation agriculture there. The Ubaid period pottery of southern Mesopotamia has been connected via <a href="/wiki/Choga_Mami" title="Choga Mami">Choga Mami</a> transitional ware to the pottery of the <a href="/wiki/Samarra" title="Samarra">Samarra</a> period culture (c. 5700‚Äì4900 BC <a href="/wiki/Radiocarbon_dating" title="Radiocarbon dating">C-14</a>) in the north, who were the first to practice a primitive form of irrigation agriculture along the middle Tigris River and its tributaries. The connection is most clearly seen at <a href="/wiki/Tell_el-%27Oueili" title="Tell el-'Oueili">Tell el-'Oueili</a> near <a href="/wiki/Larsa" title="Larsa">Larsa</a>, excavated by the French in the 1980s, where eight levels yielded pre-Ubaid pottery resembling Samarran ware. According to this theory, farming peoples spread down into southern Mesopotamia because they had developed a temple-centered social organization for mobilizing labor and technology for water control, enabling them to survive and prosper in a difficult environment.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Others have suggested a continuity of Sumerians, from the indigenous hunter-fisherfolk traditions, associated with the bifacial assemblages found on the Arabian littoral. <a href="/wiki/Juris_Zarins" title="Juris Zarins">Juris Zarins</a> believes the Sumerians may have been the people living in the Persian Gulf region before it flooded at the end of the last Ice Age.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_and_family_life">Social and family life</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Reconstructed_sumerian_headgear_necklaces_british_museum.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2468" data-file-width="1396" decoding="async" height="389" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Reconstructed_sumerian_headgear_necklaces_british_museum.JPG/220px-Reconstructed_sumerian_headgear_necklaces_british_museum.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Reconstructed_sumerian_headgear_necklaces_british_museum.JPG/330px-Reconstructed_sumerian_headgear_necklaces_british_museum.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Reconstructed_sumerian_headgear_necklaces_british_museum.JPG/440px-Reconstructed_sumerian_headgear_necklaces_british_museum.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Reconstructed_sumerian_headgear_necklaces_british_museum.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A reconstruction in the British Museum of headgear and necklaces worn by the women at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Cemetery_at_Ur" title="Royal Cemetery at Ur">Royal Cemetery at Ur</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In the early Sumerian period, the primitive pictograms suggest<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sayce_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sayce-57">[57]</a></sup> that
</p>
<ul><li>"Pottery was very plentiful, and the forms of the vases, bowls and dishes were manifold; there were special jars for honey, butter, oil and wine, which was probably made from dates. Some of the vases had pointed feet, and stood on stands with crossed legs; others were flat-bottomed, and were set on square or rectangular frames of wood. The oil-jars, and probably others also, were sealed with clay, precisely as in early <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>. Vases and dishes of stone were made in imitation of those of clay."</li>
<li>"A feathered head-dress was worn. Beds, stools and chairs were used, with carved legs resembling those of an ox. There were fire-places and fire-altars."</li>
<li>"Knives, drills, wedges and an instrument that looks like a saw were all known. While spears, bows, arrows, and daggers (but not swords) were employed in war."</li>
<li>"Tablets were used for writing purposes. Daggers with metal blades and wooden handles were worn, and copper was hammered into plates, while necklaces or collars were made of gold."</li>
<li>"Time was reckoned in lunar months."</li></ul>
<p>There is considerable evidence concerning <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sumerian_music" title="Sumerian music">Sumerian music</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lyres" title="Lyres">Lyres</a> and flutes were played, among the best-known examples being the <a href="/wiki/Lyres_of_Ur" title="Lyres of Ur">Lyres of Ur</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goss_2017_mesopotamian_flutes_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goss_2017_mesopotamian_flutes-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>Inscriptions describing the reforms of king <a href="/wiki/Urukagina" title="Urukagina">Urukagina</a> of Lagash (c. 2350 BC) say that he abolished the former custom of <a href="/wiki/Polyandry" title="Polyandry">polyandry</a> in his country, prescribing that a woman who took multiple husbands be stoned with rocks upon which her crime had been written.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:342px;max-width:342px"><div class="trow"><div class="theader">Sumerian princess (c.2150 BC)</div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:153px;max-width:153px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:226px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sumerian_princess_of_the_time_of_Gudea_circa_2150_BCE.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2337" data-file-width="1565" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Sumerian_princess_of_the_time_of_Gudea_circa_2150_BCE.jpg/151px-Sumerian_princess_of_the_time_of_Gudea_circa_2150_BCE.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Sumerian_princess_of_the_time_of_Gudea_circa_2150_BCE.jpg/227px-Sumerian_princess_of_the_time_of_Gudea_circa_2150_BCE.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Sumerian_princess_of_the_time_of_Gudea_circa_2150_BCE.jpg/302px-Sumerian_princess_of_the_time_of_Gudea_circa_2150_BCE.jpg 2x" width="151"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">Sumerian princess of the time of Gudea c. 2150 BC.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:185px;max-width:185px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:226px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sumerian_princess_of_the_time_of_Gudea_2150_BCE._Louvre_Museum_AO_295.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2022" data-file-width="1633" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Sumerian_princess_of_the_time_of_Gudea_2150_BCE._Louvre_Museum_AO_295.jpg/183px-Sumerian_princess_of_the_time_of_Gudea_2150_BCE._Louvre_Museum_AO_295.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Sumerian_princess_of_the_time_of_Gudea_2150_BCE._Louvre_Museum_AO_295.jpg/275px-Sumerian_princess_of_the_time_of_Gudea_2150_BCE._Louvre_Museum_AO_295.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Sumerian_princess_of_the_time_of_Gudea_2150_BCE._Louvre_Museum_AO_295.jpg/366px-Sumerian_princess_of_the_time_of_Gudea_2150_BCE._Louvre_Museum_AO_295.jpg 2x" width="183"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">Frontal detail.<br/>Louvre Museum AO 295.</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Sumerian culture was male-dominated and stratified. The <a href="/wiki/Code_of_Ur-Nammu" title="Code of Ur-Nammu">Code of Ur-Nammu</a>, the oldest such codification yet discovered, dating to the Ur III, reveals a glimpse at societal structure in late Sumerian law. Beneath the <i>lu-gal</i> ("great man" or king), all members of society belonged to one of two basic strata: The "<i>lu</i>" or free person, and the slave (male, <i>arad</i>; female <i>geme</i>). The son of a <i>lu</i> was called a <i>dumu-nita</i> until he married.
